<div id="review_602643978" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/602643978" />
    <a title="M. Sarki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5562041-m-sarki"><img alt="M. Sarki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580379666p2/5562041.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/602643978?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M. Sarki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="M. Sarki" href="/user/show/5562041-m-sarki">M. Sarki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781846554698" href="/book/show/16113759-a-man-in-love">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer602643978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10163775562928337745"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/90345124413/a-man-in-love-min-kamp-2-by-karl-ove-knausgard'>http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/9034512...</a><br/><br/>As I was nearing the end of <i>Volume II</i> I actually felt a bit silly and embarrassed as I looked forward to reading my customary turtle-paced six pages each morning. I used the book as part of my daily meditation as I knew there was no way I could read it like I do novels in which I am interested in and cannot help myself in finishing more than too fast. And as hard as it was for me to trudge through the almost endless Knausgård rhetoric involving changing di</span>
  <span id="freeText10163775562928337745" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/90345124413/a-man-in-love-min-kamp-2-by-karl-ove-knausgard">http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/9034512...</a><br /><br />As I was nearing the end of <i>Volume II</i> I actually felt a bit silly and embarrassed as I looked forward to reading my customary turtle-paced six pages each morning. I used the book as part of my daily meditation as I knew there was no way I could read it like I do novels in which I am interested in and cannot help myself in finishing more than too fast. And as hard as it was for me to trudge through the almost endless Knausgård rhetoric involving changing dirty diapers and idle conversation with people of no interest to him or to me it struck me constantly how there are very few writers I respect and admire that I would give the same reverence to as I seem to give this guy Knausgård. I am simply indifferent these days to child-rearing, and this is one of the major points of focus in his life as a writer. But in all his endless chatter regarding his self-inflicted mundane life, and my growing disinterest in basically everything he does but write, he seems to not care one bit what I might think of him and what he says. Knausgård himself claims he would never understand why anyone would read him either. And somehow I feel sorry for him even as he wheels his double-wide baby carriage full of cash to deposit into his local bank.<br /><br />Karl Ove Knausgård has been compared to Marcel Proust, and unfairly so it seems. It is likely because Knausgård’s completed series spans six large books. I never felt I had enough time left in my life to properly give Proust a fair reading at the expense of so many other novels I felt I still had to read. Though I have tried a bit in the past to read Proust I gave up the struggle rather quickly. I did recognize his greatness just from the little bit I read. And I happened to own quite a lovely set of his books I sold for a rather large sum a few years ago, not to mention my visiting his grave in Père Lachaise. <br /><br />Quite often the agenda-based literary types complain about Knausgård’s maleness, race, and sexual orientation being one and the same with all the other media-driven marketing stars of time past. I basically do not have any need either for elitist white man's drivel, or parenthood, daycare, or love relationships, mothers and fathers and the sins inflicted on their children, drinking, beating off, or whatever it is these complainers are talking about. I read for the words, the language, and even though Knausgård is reported to not like the English translation of his books is even more reason for me to read them. He is also a contrarian and I believe the world needs more of them in it. As for the powers-that-be and their marketing genius focussed on the white heterosexual I say, "have at it." Hardly anybody reads anymore anyway. At least not anything literary. I never have liked people with agendas, or organizations that think they know better, or works of art that complain about things as they are. I prefer, through the language, to get my socks knocked off every chance I get. Knausgård does NOT do that for me, but there is something about him that has several really smart people trying to figure out what that is, and certainly not because he is popular today with a few wannabes. Something occurs in his text that changes things, that alters something in our bodies, and there is a redemptive quality after finishing these rather long books. And don't ask me why because I don't know what it is. <br /><br />I rarely, if ever, buy a book due to its marketing. The few times I have I was extremely disappointed and swore I would never do it again. I read a lot and learn of other writers I might be interested through this process of discovery. I am involved quite intimately with like-minded readers who offer me so many book recommendations that there is no way I can possibly get to them all. I am so far removed from the marketing aspect that I feel I am on the fringe. Add that in the summer I spend four months in Michigan in a cabin with no TV and I am basically advertisement-free. And when I am home in Kentucky I watch internet TV services such as Netflix and amazon prime and again have no marketing thrown at me at all. It is only during the Super Bowl, The Kentucky Derby, and the nightly news that I am bombarded by ads for Viagra, Depends, and other unsavory delectables. And I do not watch Oprah or read her shitty magazine. So marketing does not work for me, or on me. And the readers who depend on these advertisers and blurbs to tell them what to read deserve what they get.<br /><br />There is nothing in Vol II of <i>My Struggle</i> that feels important enough for me to comment on. It is enough for me that I finished another book in this series. I have not learned much from Knausgård either. I don’t like the music he listens to or even many of the books he reads. And it is perhaps because of our age difference that he matters so little to me as a contemporary. But what I do like is his style, his indifference to what any of us might think of him, and his determination to see this project through. He is going to continue telling us his life story and we might all as well get used to it. He is a relatively big star in regards to the few people who might be reading him. He is a dark and dangerous man, and has looks that compare with an actor named Bridges. Knausgård is as stunned as some of we are at his current success. I find myself simply happy for him and despise all the jealous griping. And Knausgård is not a creep, which is saying more for him than other media darlings who seem to get far more marketing attention than he.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10163775562928337745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_814628665" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/814628665" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1826682-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252295186p2/1826682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/814628665?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/1826682-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=parenting">parenting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer814628665" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4677699806350211848">We are almost finished with Volume Two of Knausgaard’s six-volume memoir before we learn what Knausgaard is about with this huge, unwieldy thing he calls a novel. <blockquote>"Over recent year I had increasingly lost faith in literature…Fictional writing has no value, documentary narrative has no value. The only genres I saw value in, which still conferred meaning, were diaries and essays, the types of literature did not deal with narrative, that were not about anything, but just consisted of a voice, the v</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText4677699806350211848" style="display:none">We are almost finished with Volume Two of Knausgaard’s six-volume memoir before we learn what Knausgaard is about with this huge, unwieldy thing he calls a novel. <blockquote>"Over recent year I had increasingly lost faith in literature…Fictional writing has no value, documentary narrative has no value. The only genres I saw value in, which still conferred meaning, were diaries and essays, the types of literature did not deal with narrative, that were not about anything, but just consisted of a voice, the voice of your own personality, a life, a face, a gaze you could meet. What is a work of art if not the gaze of another person?"</blockquote> Does that not mean that art is subjective, and in the eyes of the beholder only? He is still asking questions rather than answering them in this staggeringly discursive but surprisingly readable set of books about his life: "I wanted to get as close to life as possible." We sit, like Geir, his best friend and sounding board, hearing his explanations, and bringing our own understanding to his novel/memoir/quest. The writer Karl Ove places some observations about Karl Ove the narrator in the mouth of Geir:<blockquote>"You’re an arch-protestant…If you have some success, generally something others would have died for, you just cross it off in the ledger. You’re not happy about anything. When you’re at one with yourself, which you are almost all of the time, you’re much more disciplined than me…Your ideal is the innocent, innocence…what you lust for is innocence and this is an impossible equation. Lust and innocence can never be compatible."</blockquote><br />Since reading <a href="http://thebowedbookshelf.blogspot.com/2015/03/my-struggle-volume-i-by-karl-ove.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Min Kamp Volume One</a>, I watched a number of interviews of Knausgaard. Knausgaard tells us in Volume Two he cannot stop himself from accepting invitations to speak about himself and his book, despite his terror and despite the oftentimes mediocre write-ups. His sense of worthlessness and feelings of intimidation (he says these feelings are rooted in Norwegian culture and his own upbringing) are clear from what he says, writes, and does because, he says, he has revealed the darkest, most shameful things about himself and his family and friends. One might understand, therefore, his reluctance to be in public answering questions about his motivations were it not for the vast number of critics coming down on the side of celebration and awe upon the publication of the linked books. This praise he “crosses off” the income side of the ledger, leaving him desperate and despondent. Well, okay, if that’s how you want to play it. I can heap criticism on his head, too. A little bit of whip-play, eh?<br /><br />We must ask ourselves why we care. How much of this is fiction and does it matter? Are we as close to life as possible—a little reality show for the bookish set, the novel-minded?<br /><br />The author Karl Ove tells us this book is about love, and readers might agree. Love in its imperfections, in the imperfections of the lovers, in the circumstances, in the <i>choices one makes</i>, and in the choices one doesn’t. Love between parents and children and children and parents and between the parents and between friends; Love that is not blind, but alternately tender and vengeful, accepting and unyielding. There is a love of writing here, too, of the lost-in-the-dream flow of writing, of the can’t-wait-to-get-back-to-it addictiveness of creating something unique. Which is how we know it probably is fiction. Karl Ove is creating, not just recording. He “finally gets to tell the story” and it is his story. Not objective, but subjective. Fictive, at least in part. We learn the tiniest detail of his child’s outdoor clothing and how to cook a meal of potatoes, steak and broccoli, but we will never hear the voice of his father except through Karl Ove the narrator. What difference does it make? None. Memoirs and fiction often trade places.<br /><br />Regarding the larger question of whether or not it is literature, I sidestep: that will answer itself over time. The books have their amusements and instruction, for we read so deeply about others’ decisions, successes and downfalls, hidden secrets and cracks in the façade, as well as about Scandinavia, lest anyone think I forgot the cultural context. But were I pressed, I would guess I am reading something resembling an old-fashioned “confession” perhaps like St Augustine’s <i>Confessions</i> written when he was in his early 40s, considered by some to be the first autobiography in the western world, and in which Augustine regrets his sinful life. <i>Confessions</i> ran to <i>thirteen</i> volumes and was meant to be read aloud. In at least <a href="http://www.londonreviewbookshop.co.uk/blog/2014/11/video-karl-ove-knausgaard-talks-with-andrew-ohagan" rel="nofollow noopener">one interview</a>, Karl Ove tells us he read his book aloud to an unnamed friend. <br /><br />St. Augustine and St. Thomas Aquinas, whom Karl Ove references, had a long standing disagreement about how best to understand the divine. Confused, sinful, ascetic (rejecting the world) Karl Ove (Augustine?) talks repeatedly and deeply with organized, controlled, disciplined Geir (Aquinas?) who embraces the world, even to the point of travelling to witness the war in Iraq. We listen; we wonder. <br /><br />All this is to say nothing of Hitler, who is mentioned twice I think in this book, and once in Volume One. Perhaps in Volume Three we will have three references? Of that, stay tuned. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4677699806350211848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1270255094" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1270255094" />
    <a title="Caterina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5558460-caterina"><img alt="Caterina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487431115p2/5558460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1270255094?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caterina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caterina" href="/user/show/5558460-caterina">Caterina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781935744825" href="/book/show/16057602-my-struggle">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Stephen Wood</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=bio-or-memoir">bio-or-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=novels-novellas">novels-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=internat-setting">internat-setting</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=norwegian-author">norwegian-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=norwegian-engl-tran">norwegian-engl-tran</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=set-in-norway">set-in-norway</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=set-in-sweden">set-in-sweden</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=archipelago-books">archipelago-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5558460-caterina?shelf=cultural-observations">cultural-observations</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1270255094" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10164197846803633523">Maybe because it deals with the sweetness and struggles of love and marriage, I found My Struggle, Book Two: A Man in Love even more compelling -- and enjoyable -- than Book One: A Death in the Family. Together, they are a breathtakingly honest, intimate portrayal of the ordinary lived experience and interior life of someone who ruminates about everything -- a self-absorption that somehow transcends self. We read to get out of the self, Karl Ove claims, and that rings true to me - so there's som</span>
  <span id="freeText10164197846803633523" style="display:none">Maybe because it deals with the sweetness and struggles of love and marriage, I found My Struggle, Book Two: A Man in Love even more compelling -- and enjoyable -- than Book One: A Death in the Family. Together, they are a breathtakingly honest, intimate portrayal of the ordinary lived experience and interior life of someone who ruminates about everything -- a self-absorption that somehow transcends self. We read to get out of the self, Karl Ove claims, and that rings true to me - so there's something paradoxical about this whole venture. <br /><br />The subject of Book Two is Karl Ove Knausgaard's falling in love, marriage, and family life with the writer Linda Bostrom, following his unexplained abandonment and divorce of his first wife Tonje, with whom, we gather, he could no longer stand to stay. When the novel begins, Karl Ove and Linda are struggling with three small, willful children who are showing their parents (almost comically) how very little in life it is possible to control. Certainly not one's own tiny children! -- And not one's own parents — three out of four of the couple's parents struggle or struggled with alcoholism or mental illness. Not one's spouse - not even, fully, oneself - though one can of course try and try again. This turns out to be one of the novel's themes. When a book tour takes Karl Ove back to Norway from Sweden, the sublime sight of the mountains and sky triggers this reaction, one of my favorite passages:<br /><br /><blockquote>This was beyond our comprehension. We might believe that our world embraced everything, we might do our thing down here on the beach, drive around in cars, phone each other and chat, visit one another, eat and drink and sit indoors imbibing the faces and opinions and the fates of those appearing on the TV screen in this strange, semi-artificial symbiosis we inhabited and lull ourselves for longer and longer, year upon year, into thinking that this was all there was, but if on the odd occasion we were to raise our gaze to this, the only possible thought was one of incomprehension and impotence, for in fact how small and trivial was the world we allowed ourselves to be lulled by? Yes, of course, the dramas we saw were magnificent, the images we internalized sublime and sometimes also apocalyptic, but be honest, slaves, what part did we play in them?<br /><br />None.<br /><br />But the stars twinkle above our heads, the sun shines, the grass grows and the earth, yes, the earth, it swallows all life and eradicates all vestiges of it, spews out new life in a cascade of limbs and eyes, leaves and nails, hair and tails, cheeks and fur and guts, and swallows it up again. And what we never really comprehend, or don't want to comprehend, is that this happens outside us, that we ourselves have no part in it, that we are only that which grows and dies, as blind as the waves in the sea are blind.</blockquote><br /><br />Some of the book's most moving passages involved KOK learning to see and understand and love his own parents, even his once-hated father, in light of new information and his own experiences as a husband and father and middle-aged man whose life is no longer in the future, but now, in all its banality -- and in that, paradoxically, another kind of power. The ordinary experiences of life, however, include also the extraordinary, the unexplainable, those moments of revelation, of sublimity, found in nature and art and love. <br /><br />It amazes me that although (in interviews) KOK calls these novels, not memoirs, and admits to inventing all the details and conversations, these books nevertheless come across as truth, as real, shared human experience. At the end of the book he further explains his motivation as a writer: to him, fiction has become a sort of mass-production, always at a distance -- the same uniform distance -- from life. He wants to do something different, get closer. I think he succeeds. Both Books One and Two seem to transform every mundane detail of life into something worthy to be relished. This extends even to mundane ugliness -- and he doesn't flatter himself. For example, when Linda - heavily pregnant and subject to extreme anxiety about losing the baby, begs him to ask his friend to slow down as they speed across the water in a boat -- a very rough ride -- Karl Ove refuses to do it, ignoring his wife's suffering. On occasions like this I found myself saying: “Karl Ove! You say you want to be a good person so stop being an asshole!” Then I would remember some similar thing I might have done. On these occasions he often finds himself filled with shame, wondering why he can't seem to be the good person he thinks he wants to be. "Why is that so hard?"<br /><br />Here is perhaps the novel's most important and paradoxical effect: early on, KOK laments his inability to remain present to his own life, his desire always to escape. "This is my struggle," he says. (Oh, how I sympathize.) In an act of desperation more with regard to his writing than his life, he cuts loose and begins to write this almost excessively detailed narrative of the ordinary. And although he continues to lament that he is still not living life, but only writing about life -- yet somehow he seems to be providing a medicine — whether antidote or palliative, I don't know — to the modern condition, maybe the human condition. Somehow this seems to be exactly what I need -- what many of us need -- to allow it to be ok, to be good to be present, to be right here, right now.<br /><br />But how can a novel without much of a plot be so compelling? This volume focuses intensely on evolving or at least continually changing experience/understanding of love and marriage and the rocky road of family relationships. In a lot of fiction, that subject is treated as a drama with a plot leading somewhere -- divorce or lasting happy marriage, say -- and details are chosen accordingly, to make it look as if that's where things were always heading. But in My Struggle, it's more like real life: no plot, just ongoing cycles of tenderness, conflicts, resolutions to do better, the possibility alive in every moment to make the decision to love, to stay -- or not -- and, in every moment, those surprising revelations and epiphanies that lead to deeper understanding of one another. There is something biblical about that structure -- people repeatedly screwing up, thinking about leaving, trying again, learning to love, learning to live. Perhaps missing, or at least not explicit, in this account, is any sense of covenant. Or it may be there implicitly in the marriage, but I'm not so sure about that - Karl Ove seemed to express no explicit sense of commitment "for better or worse." <br /><br />There’s so much more to be said that every review of this book could talk about something different. Another aspect I enjoyed was Knausgaard’s interest in exploring non-modern themes; he’s inspired by biblical literature; he’s constantly working out what masculinity, being a man, might mean today versus what it meant to previous generations; he talks about things no one else does -- because no one else does. So refreshing. I look forward to the next installments.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10164197846803633523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2236273758" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2236273758" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2236273758?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789044522044" href="/book/show/15919595-liefde">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=autobiography">autobiography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=scandinavian-literature">scandinavian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=norway">norway</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=sweden">sweden</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2236273758" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11353566121836259553">What’s the matter with Knausgard?, what game is he playing with us? Part 1 of his series bears the title "Father", and that father certainly is present, but rather as a threatening shadow than as an acting person (the last third of that book is situated around the days after his death), and in this Part 2 the title is "Love", but almost constantly it is about divorces between people and quarrels between Karl Ove and his wife Linda. <br/><br/>Of course, this second part also contains very endearing passage</span>
  <span id="freeText11353566121836259553" style="display:none">What’s the matter with Knausgard?, what game is he playing with us? Part 1 of his series bears the title "Father", and that father certainly is present, but rather as a threatening shadow than as an acting person (the last third of that book is situated around the days after his death), and in this Part 2 the title is "Love", but almost constantly it is about divorces between people and quarrels between Karl Ove and his wife Linda. <br /><br />Of course, this second part also contains very endearing passages: about falling in love, about the caring relationship between Karl Ove and his children, but the focus is on the inability that the author sees in himself to really love and give priority to the people he actually loves. Once again, Karl Ove is portrayed as a rather unsympathetic person, who drinks lavishly, does not stick to what he agreed to, and is engrossed by nostalgia and restlessness. There is only one passion that always returns and he stays true to: writing; but even in that one passion there’s continual doubt, whether it is really good what he puts on paper, and he constantly compares himself (to his disadvantage) to other, greater Scandinavian writers.<br /><br />As in the first part, long drawn-out descriptions of trivial activities (shopping, taking care of the children, walking the streets, table conversations) alternate with sometimes pertinent considerations about parenting, intense poignant scenes (the whole delivery scene, for example), in-depth character analyses of friends and family members and atmospheric place descriptions. And then there are the striking incongruences: contradictions between scenes in the first part and those in this part (for example, the days before the birth of his first child); does Knausgard consciously indicate that he is an unreliable narrator? Also some quality difference in the writing is very noticeable: some passages are clearly written sloppily, hanging around without obvious meaning, while others are real stylistic gems.<br />Knausgard continues to intrigue, but I keep on wondering where all this is heading to.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11353566121836259553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_114718410" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114718410" />
    <a title="Flaneurette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4094158-flaneurette"><img alt="Flaneurette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359892068p2/4094158.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/114718410?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Flaneurette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Flaneurette" href="/user/show/4094158-flaneurette">Flaneurette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4094158-flaneurette?shelf=bokm-l">bokm-l</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4094158-flaneurette?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114718410" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9894317143103588839">Great writing by a great author with a great voice, yet not the great literature Knausgård is capable of producing. I heard an interview with Knausgård on Leonard Lopate which I think explains it all. He reported that he wrote and never looked back. He has not even read what he has written. I did notice that he is quite wordy in this volume, there is no economy of ideas and reflections, a certain lack of editing is apparent, not to mention that one very annoying spelling mistake. But honestly I </span>
  <span id="freeText9894317143103588839" style="display:none">Great writing by a great author with a great voice, yet not the great literature Knausgård is capable of producing. I heard an interview with Knausgård on Leonard Lopate which I think explains it all. He reported that he wrote and never looked back. He has not even read what he has written. I did notice that he is quite wordy in this volume, there is no economy of ideas and reflections, a certain lack of editing is apparent, not to mention that one very annoying spelling mistake. But honestly I did not mind any of these. The author's life is not that much more interesting than the lives of others, it is his honesty and unfiltered accounts brought by his beautiful language that makes this a compelling read. Of course, his critiques say that Knausgård is just an egotistical prick with an enormous need for exposure. After having read this book, I truly doubt it, quite the contrary, he seems to dread publicity and any public appearance but is searching deep in himself to find universal truths about being a man, human, an artist. I cannot see how critiques would at all enjoy any real-life literary accounts of the things that matter in life. Their loss, because this book increased my sensitivity towards everyday life and how we so often fail to see the significance of the words that fall between us, dirty dishes cleaned or left piling up, the noises our kids make and how our neighbours react to our very presence. It also made me appreciate the many influences other artists and thinkers have had on Knausgård's writing, wonderfully woven into causal beers and phone calls with his mate. Great minds have never been portrayed with such an ease without ending up shallow. <br /><br />I initially gave this one 4 stars but changed my review to 5. It is so near perfect and I cannot wait to read the other four volumes. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9894317143103588839" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1638667297" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1638667297" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18218857-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362876012p2/18218857.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1638667297?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/18218857-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374534158" href="/book/show/18490588-my-struggle">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18218857-marc?shelf=translated-so-me-can-read">translated-so-me-can-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18218857-marc?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1638667297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2273795277774299079">In chronological terms, Book 2 deals with Karl Ove's falling in love, becoming a father, dealing with fame, and searching for a new writing project. It is hard to crystallize the appeal of this book and yet it is magnetic--for me, it's as if Knausgaard pulls you into his life. And because his life is very much about the search for happiness and self-acceptance, you feel that it is your life, as well. There's a certain futility to it all because you know that humans are not wired for this type of</span>
  <span id="freeText2273795277774299079" style="display:none">In chronological terms, Book 2 deals with Karl Ove's falling in love, becoming a father, dealing with fame, and searching for a new writing project. It is hard to crystallize the appeal of this book and yet it is magnetic--for me, it's as if Knausgaard pulls you into his life. And because his life is very much about the search for happiness and self-acceptance, you feel that it is your life, as well. There's a certain futility to it all because you know that humans are not wired for this type of happiness. I don't mean to say that we can't be happy, but that there is no perpetual state of happiness that one can get to by arranging external conditions just so, or accomplishing the right set of achievements. We're kind of caught between the desire for the safety that certainty and rootedness claim to promise, and the very real need to grow, which requires change and movement. I think these two, often divergent human impulses, define Karl Ove's struggle in the book and they're very much a part of why and how humans have managed to survive. Happiness is relative. And it's even worse if it's sustained because then it becomes commonplace. Status quo. And you have a new baseline from which to try and feel happier again. Also, happiness is kind of dangerous--it leads to complacency, but I digress...<br /><br />Karl Ove tries to balance these two forces together. He tries to live in the moment, to be a good person, but also to strive for artistic ideals and output. He is compelled to write, but must balance this with changing diapers and being a husband. He feels we're mired in so much fiction in so many forms these days that the true life of the individual may be literature's only avenue for exploration and experimentation. Knausgaard has invited the reader along to be a part of this struggle, to be a part of the writing, as it's happening. It's like the reader gets to work and think through the answers alongside Karl Ove, all the while knowing there may be no answers. Or, no one answer.<br /><br />And the writing always does a wonderful job naturally ebbing and flowing between the mundane and the sublime, the practical and the aesthetic. It's time to buy groceries or plan dinner, but in the back of the mind, you're wondering whether the agricultural revolution was the greatest curse or the greatest gift to mankind. That daily struggle for survival--the search for food--is gone and leaves in its wake a kind of restlessness. Modern life deals in symbols and indirect relations to such a degree that living in the moment is a lost art. Many of life's dangers have been removed or diminished to such a degree that to feel "alive" people put themselves in danger willingly (sports, hobbies that depend on adrenalin or danger, even dating or befriending people we know aren't good for us).<br /><br />I very much like books that make me think, that get me dreaming or calculating or struggling with my own perceptions. Knausgaard does that to me. And he's so painfully open about himself. So self-critical in the ways many of us are. You laugh at how violently upset he gets just taking his daughter to a music class--it becomes this kind of symbolic emasculating event (one of many where he's struggling with what he thinks it means to be a man and how he never lives up to that notion; a notion that seems almost reactionary as if the price of gender equality and fairness were his masculinity itself). Karl Ove is moved by various painters and writers, has wonderful philosophical discussions with his friend Geir all the while losing his cell phone on a train, drinking himself silly from time to time, and trying to be a good person while still having some sort of personal boundaries (he berates himself quite often for catering to others simply to avoid conflict, as a way of seeking favor, or because he simply wants to be liked--it's all terribly human, which is why it's all terribly engrossing).<br /><br />Along the way, he tackles the homogenizaton of culture and place, a seeming disdain for fat or ugly people, dealing with in-laws, fine arts paintings that move him to tears, awkward social moments, and trying to do too much.<br /><br />In many ways, the character Karl Ove is like an emotionally clumsy giant. Like most adults, he's just a child stuck in a grownup's body trying to stumble through life. He's brilliant and foolish, thoughtful and impulsive. He needs isolation and human connection, but struggles to find balance. Don't we all?<br />--------------------------------------------------------<br />Words I Learned While Reading This Book:<br /><a href="http://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/titivate" rel="nofollow noopener">titivate</a> | <a href="http://www.thefreedictionary.com/misology" rel="nofollow noopener">misology</a> | <a href="http://www.dictionary.com/browse/cannula" rel="nofollow noopener">cannula</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2273795277774299079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13381709898006365818" style="display:none">First of all, considering with the name of book, it is not a love story.<br />A good slice from one Norwegian author who lives in Sweden. He has large family and has have intellectual society in Sweden. At the beginning of story, it is detail explained him and his daughter relation. There are interesting comparisons between Norway and Sweden. Actually, I have not any idea before read about this issue. Also, there is very good birthday celebration section in the story. People can see also inner world of one new married man in the story. I highly recommended this book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13381709898006365818" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1599648570" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1599648570" />
    <a title="Leo Robertson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7619789-leo-robertson"><img alt="Leo Robertson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571602096p2/7619789.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1599648570?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leo Robertson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leo Robertson" href="/user/show/7619789-leo-robertson">Leo Robertson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781846554704" href="/book/show/16113751-a-man-in-love">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7619789-leo-robertson?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1599648570" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1198786446261554093">Just read it.<br/><br/>Some disjointed thoughts and thrown-in quotes below— I was going to write, ‘In case you need convincing’, but why would you listen to me? Don’t you have friends? You don’t have friends who <i>read</i>? Ouch! Then don’t read this: you have to go get some friends who read ffs. For the good of your immortal soul! Or something that exists!<br/><br/>Knausgaard is no hero. He paints no mawkish and sentimental view of life as it is really experienced. He’s just a cantankerous everyman. Maybe the everiest t</span>
  <span id="freeText1198786446261554093" style="display:none">Just read it.<br /><br />Some disjointed thoughts and thrown-in quotes below— I was going to write, ‘In case you need convincing’, but why would you listen to me? Don’t you have friends? You don’t have friends who <i>read</i>? Ouch! Then don’t read this: you have to go get some friends who read ffs. For the good of your immortal soul! Or something that exists!<br /><br />Knausgaard is no hero. He paints no mawkish and sentimental view of life as it is really experienced. He’s just a cantankerous everyman. Maybe the everiest there ever was.<br /><br /><i>So the life I led was not my own. I tried to make it mine, this was my struggle, because of course I wanted it, but I failed, the longing for something else undermined all my efforts.</i><br /><br />Yup.<br /><br /><i>I stopped for a few seconds by the newspaper stand wondering whether to buy the two evening papers, the two biggest publications. Reading them was like emptying a bag of rubbish over your head. Now and then I did buy them, when it felt as though a bit more rubbish up there wouldn’t make any difference. But not today.</i><br /><br />Yeah!<br /><br /><i>I was depressed as I left, I was so incredibly sick of having everything inside me, being unable to say the simplest thing to anyone.<br />It passed.</i><br /><br />Always does!<br /><br /><i>What was it that Rilke wrote? That music raised him out of himself, and never returned him to where it had found him, but to a deeper place, somewhere in the unfinished?<br />It was unlikely he had been thinking about country music.</i><br /><br />Contrast the above with a quote from Joyce’s <i>Ulysses</i>:<br /><br /><i>Trams passed one another, ingoing, outgoing, clanging. Useless words. Things go on same, day after day: squads of police marching out, back: trams in, out. Those two loonies mooching about. Dignam carted off. Mina Purefoy swollen belly on a bed groaning to have a child tugged out of her. One born every second somewhere. Other dying every second. Since I fed the birds five minutes. Three hundred kicked the bucket. Other three hundred born, washing the blood off, all are washed in the blood of the lamb, bawling maaaaaa.<br />Cityful passing away, other cityful coming, passing away too: other coming on, passing on. Houses, lines of houses, streets, miles of pavements, piledup bricks, stones. Changing hands. This owner, that. Landlord never dies they say. Other steps into his shoes when he gets his notice to quit. They buy the place up with gold and still they have all the gold. Swindle in it somewhere. Piled up in cities, worn away age after age. Pyramids in sand. Built on bread and onions. Slaves Chinese wall. Babylon. Big stones left. Round towers. Rest rubble, sprawling suburbs, jerrybuilt. Kerwan's mushroom houses built of breeze. Shelter, for the night.<br />No-one is anything.<br />This is the very worst hour of the day. Vitality. Dull, gloomy: hate this hour. Feel as if I had been eaten and spewed.</i><br /><br />In both we get the same idea that the stream of consciousness is a flow of vaguely connected thoughts, some important, some mundane, some profound, some silly. Some taking in the environment, others borne of introspection. I think Knausgaard does it better. I wonder if it is that Joyce’s pioneering of the style required it to be so radical to shock readers into seeing what literature could do; Knausgaard tosses off his sentences and never gives the impression— unlike Joyce— that he’s performing. Unlike Joyce, Knausgaard has no desire to reassure you that every life is its own profound synecdoche, the infinite in its details, filled with rich inner life, implicitly participating in history, the fountainhead of the monomyth! Knausgaard is more like, if you think reaching age X means you’ll know more, you’re in for a ride, you piece of shit. I know which of the two messages I relate to more! I know which reflects my own experience of life more: go to this job, is this my job? Who’s that? Why was I mean to him? Oh, I just forgot to eat. I’ll apologise. If I remember. Could he even tell? Probably not. Forgot the mince! What am I doing with my life?! Everyone else seems happy and they appreciate you: why don’t you appreciate them? You ungrateful POS! Must meditate more: be mindful. Don’t eat bacon. Fuck that! Could die tomorrow. Married! I can’t get fat? What’s the point? Let’s burn this life to the ground just to see what happens! Oh God don’t take this life away from me! And on and on and on….<br /><br />Most Norwegians are sick of Knausgaard, by the way. If you’re wondering why nine tenths of the country doesn’t own a Knausgaard book it’s because the tenth that does won’t shut the fuck up about him. His omnipresence on bookstands, in newspapers, in your local events centre giving his opinion on who-cares-what-he-thinks, then later writing some bitter essay about what a dreary and pointless experience it was for him, has many vowing not to pick up his books. Personally I’m glad because their dissent got me into it. But it’s a shame also, because it gives you the impression that this is Capital L Literature. That is, its very mention etches MUST READ upon your synapses, scarring them and muting other impulses that might lead you to something you might actually want to read instead of what has been popularly prescribed to you. But this is not Literature: it’s just literature. I don’t like Literature; I love literature.<br /><br /><i>’This will have consequences,’ she said.<br />‘I don’t give a shit,’ I said. ‘Who do you think they’ll believe? A lonely alcoholic Russian or an established couple with a small child?’<br />Then I closed the door and went back in. Linda stood looking at me from the living-room doorway. I walked past without a glance.<br />‘That wasn’t perhaps the wisest move,’ I said. ‘But it felt good.’<br />‘I can imagine.’</i><br /><br />The point of a marriage is more or less to drag the person you love most around with you everywhere. A caveat of this— to name but one!— is that they will catch you being petty as hell. Needs humour. Above is proof.<br /><br /><i>Ladies and gentlemen. I don’t give a shit about you, I don’t give a shit about the book I’ve written, I don’t give a shit if it wins a prize or not, all I want is to write more. So what am I doing here? I allowed myself to feel flattered, I had a moment of weakness, I have lots of them, but now this is the end of feeling flattered and having moments of weakness. To mark the occasion in a suitably unambiguous way I’ve brought along a few newspapers with me. I’m going to place them on the floor in front of the rostrum and have a shit. I’ve saved it up for a few days so that I can make the point with force. So here we go. Right. Oooh. There we are. Now I’ll just wipe my arse and that’s that. May I now pass over to the second nominee? Thank you.</i><br /><br />Imagine missing out on gold like the above because you mistook this book for being Important instead of important!<br /><br />Occasionally your consciousness slips out of alignment with his:<br /><br /><i>Don’t believe you are anybody.<br />Do not bloody believe you are somebody.<br />Because you are not. Your’e just a smug mediocre little shit.<br />Do not believe that you’re anything special. Do not believe that you’re worth anything, because you aren’t. You’re just a little shit.<br />So keep your head down and work, you little shit. Then at least you’ll get something out of it. Shut your mouth, keep your head down, work and know that you’re not worth a shit.<br />This, more or less, was what I had learned.<br />This was the sum of all my experience.<br />This was the only true bloody thought I’d ever had.</i><br /><br />But you don’t stop being entertained.<br /><br /><i>Whenever I had done an interview and there was something in the interview I hadn’t said or what I had said was cast in a different light, I moved heaven and earth to change it. If it wasn’t possible, my self-image burned with shame. The fact that despite all this I went on giving interviews, and once again sat facing a journalist somewhere, was the result of my desire for flattery being stronger than both my fear of looking like an idiot and any ideals of quality I had, as well as acknowledging that it was important for the books to reach a readership.<br /><br />You could always get money, and pure existence, one where you stand face to face with a life-threatening emergency or peril, had been completely eliminated. This was the thinking that had spawned a culture in which the greatest mediocrities, warm and with a well-fed stomach, trumpeted their cheap platitudes, thus allowing writers to be worshipped as if Virgil himself were sitting on the sofa and telling us whether he had used a pen or a typewriter or a computer and what times of the day he wrote.<br /><br />I felt like disappearing into the depths of a forest somewhere, building myself a log cabin and staying there, far from civilisation and gazing into a fire. People, who needs people?</i><br /><br />True that!<br /><br />Or as they say in Norwegian: <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1198786446261554093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1387642406" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1387642406" />
    <a title="Кремена Михайлова" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7907888"><img alt="Кремена Михайлова" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1474702238p2/7907888.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1387642406?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Кремена Михайлова" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Кремена Михайлова" href="/user/show/7907888">Кремена Михайлова</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1387642406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14546398780413912063">Мъжете да я четат два пъти.<br/>Жените да я четат с двойни очи.<br/>(Децата да продължат да виждат/казват истината.)<br/><br/>Заради всичко „грешно“, заради всичко „правилно“.<br/>Заради силата на писането. И липсата на огради.<br/><br/>Хаотичният строеж ме впримчи здраво: напред-назад в годините; насам-натам от тема на тема; от „поток на съзнанието“ към пространни диалози; от тривиалното до философското, от личното до глобалното. Все бях дълбоко „вътре“ (дори когато не бях съгласна с някои виждания на автора). Запитах се дали на</span>
  <span id="freeText14546398780413912063" style="display:none">Мъжете да я четат два пъти.<br />Жените да я четат с двойни очи.<br />(Децата да продължат да виждат/казват истината.)<br /><br />Заради всичко „грешно“, заради всичко „правилно“.<br />Заради силата на писането. И липсата на огради.<br /><br />Хаотичният строеж ме впримчи здраво: напред-назад в годините; насам-натам от тема на тема; от „поток на съзнанието“ към пространни диалози; от тривиалното до философското, от личното до глобалното. Все бях дълбоко „вътре“ (дори когато не бях съгласна с някои виждания на автора). Запитах се дали на моменти Кнаусгор не е допуснал излишности в желанието си да опише детайлно всичко, което мисли или е преживял. Но в доста подобни задълбавания потъвах, загубвайки представа къде съм и колко време е минало. <br />(Като в първата книга пак основното ми притеснение е само едно – публично достояние да стават толкова съкровени неща, свързани с негови малко близки и много близки хора.) <br /><br />Не зная дали е новаторство това, защото не съм чела достатъчно подобни книги, но ми се струва, че My Struggle не e просто автобиография. Въздействието може да се е засилило и от това, че освен близостта във връзка с отглеждането на децата/домакинството („копирани“ мои преживявания), възприемах Карл Уве Кнаусгор като себе си – чувстващият тип „несоциален темерут“. <br /><br />Отношенията с жена му са акцент в тази част и ми направиха най-силно впечатление (особено когато 4-годишното им дете казва нещо от рода на „Вие двамата винаги сте ядосани“); и все пак най-забавното за мен – бърборенето в компания… :)))<br /><br /><i>„I could definitely have offered an opinion about that, but I didn’t – small talk is one of the infinite number of talents I haven’t mastered – so I sat nodding at what was said, as usual, smiling when others smiled, while ardently wishing myself miles away.”</i><br /><br />__________________________________<br /><br />Right after finishing the book I am stunned and unable to utter a word but copy some abstracts from ‘A Man in Love’. It's not just about his stories, it's about his wording, text construction and wide span.<br /><br /><i>“I, for my part, never looked forward to anything except the moment the office door closed behind me and I was alone and able to write.”<br /><br />“How can you sit there receiving applause when you know that what you have done is not good enough? I had one opportunity. I had to cut all my ties with the flattering, thoroughly corrupt world of culture in which everyone, every single little upstart, was for sale, cut all my ties with the vacuous TV and newspaper world, sit down in a room and read in earnest, not contemporary literature but literature of the highest quality, and then write as if my life depended on it. For twenty years if need be.<br />But I couldn’t grasp the opportunity. I had a family and I owed it to them to be there. I had friends. And I had a weakness in my character which meant that I would say yes, yes, when I wanted to say no, no, which was so afraid of hurting others, which was so afraid of conflict and which was so afraid of not being liked that it could forgo all principles, all dreams, all opportunities, everything that smacked of truth, to prevent this happening. <br />I was a whore. This was the only suitable term.”<br /><br />“A hut, skiing with friends, that was something I had never done. While I was at gymnas and for a couple of years into the course at university, this had been a sore point. I barely had any friends. And the few I had didn’t know each other. Now I was too old to bother about that sort of thing, but nevertheless I did feel a stab of pain, on behalf of the old me, as it were.”<br /><br />“As always after long interviews I felt empty, drained like a ditch.<br />As always, it felt as though I had betrayed myself.” <br /><br />“I was preoccupied, to an unusually high degree, by being liked, and always had been, ever since I was small. I had attached huge importance to what other people thought about me ever since I was seven. When newspapers showed some interest in what I was doing and who I was, it was, on the one hand, confirmation that I was liked and therefore something part of me accepted with great pleasure while, on the other, it became an almost unmanageable problem because it was no longer possible to control other people’s opinions of me, for the simple reason that I no longer knew them, no longer saw them. So whenever I had done an interview and there was something in the interview I hadn’t said or what I had said was cast in a different light, I moved heaven and earth to change it. If it wasn’t possible, my self-image burned with shame. The fact that despite all this I went on giving interviews, and once again sat facing a journalist somewhere, was the result of my desire for flattery being stronger than both my fear of looking an idiot and any ideals of quality I had, as well as acknowledging that it was important for the books to reach a readership.”<br /><br />“But turning away didn’t help. It didn’t help to cover my face with my hands. It didn’t help to walk towards the hall. It was so overwhelming, I was sobbing with such abandon, all the sluices were open.“<br /><br />“More often than not it was me who cooked when we had guests. Not so much because I liked doing it but because it gave me something to hide behind. I could stay in the kitchen when they arrived, poke my head in and say hello, carry on cooking in the kitchen, hidden, until the food was ready to serve and I had to appear. But even then I could hide behind something: a glass had to be filled with wine, another with water, I could take care of that and the instant the first course was finished I could clear the table and set it for the next.”<br /><br />“Taking the initiative – suddenly talking about something I had experienced or thought in an attempt to get the conversation going – was beyond me.”<br /><br />“What once had irked me, walking through the town with a buggy, was now history, forgotten and outlandish, as I pushed a shabby buggy with three children on board around the streets, often with two or three shopping bags dangling from one hand, deep furrows carved in my brow and down my cheeks, and eyes that burned with a vacant ferocity I had long lost any contact with. I no longer bothered about the potentially feminised nature of what I did; now it was a question of getting the children to wherever we had to go, with no sit-down strikes or refusals to go any further or any other ideas they could dream up to thwart my wishes for an easy morning or afternoon. Once a crowd of Japanese tourists stopped on the other side of the street and pointed at me, as though I were the ringmaster of some circus parade or something. They pointed. There you can see a Scandinavian man! Look, and tell your grandchildren what you saw!”<br /><br />“Fictional writing has no value, documentary narrative has no value. The only genres I saw value in, which still conferred meaning, were diaries and essays, the types of literature that did not deal with narrative, that were not about anything, but just consisted of a voice, the voice of your own personality, a life, a face, a gaze you could meet. What is a work of art if not the gaze of another person? Not directed above us, nor beneath us, but at the same height as our own gaze. Art cannot be experienced collectively, nothing can, art is something you are alone with. You meet its gaze alone.”<br /><br />“A life is simple to understand, the elements that determine it are few. In mine there were two. My father and the fact that I had never belonged anywhere.”</i><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14546398780413912063" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer1505155917374305015">dnf @ 23%<br/>probably the most boring book i've ever tried reading. this pretentious, self-hating man reminds me of the worst kind of people you meet at a party. who told him it was a good idea to take 7,000 pages to write his autobiography? he was like 40 when we published it, it's 7,000 pages of HALF A LIFE. it boggles my mind. <br/><br/>anyway, you gotta fill up that page count somehow, so everything gets drawn out. instead of showing one or a few examples of how being a parent to young kids is the worst, </span>
  <span id="freeText1505155917374305015" style="display:none">dnf @ 23%<br />probably the most boring book i've ever tried reading. this pretentious, self-hating man reminds me of the worst kind of people you meet at a party. who told him it was a good idea to take 7,000 pages to write his autobiography? he was like 40 when we published it, it's 7,000 pages of HALF A LIFE. it boggles my mind. <br /><br />anyway, you gotta fill up that page count somehow, so everything gets drawn out. instead of showing one or a few examples of how being a parent to young kids is the worst, most embarrassing job in existance, KOK shows us like 100 pages of it. it was like reading the extended diary of deeply unlikeable and boring person. i actually love unlikeable charcaters! but this man went to the supermarket and spent a paragraph describing how fat and disgusting the cashier was.<br /><br />400+ more pages of this? yeah, no thanks</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('P3qViWO5njU8B/nQN8u3bTlQsgUuXpf1+xQOui65Zcza/GBG72IR40YTVzjoBbsV31DCy5gZpuvwVkIhbPs+mw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('WQIi4CB3MJquhWxs6bx7RObQGXeIr302uojb63OA3hO8hNcvrKy/TNSRwoQ2cnc8ANBpuT7oTCixypdwMcKFRA==')})
  }
//]]>

Behandlingen är vanlig och anses vede<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16795852604113564318\" style=\"display:none\">Periodvis mellan 2013 och 2017 är författaren tvångsinlagd på en psykiatrisk avdelning, där hon utsätts för upprepade serier ECT-behandlingar, det vill säga elektricitet som framkallar ett slags epileptisk kramp. Behandlingen är vanlig och anses vedertagen för många psykiska åkommor, men forskare är ännu oense om dess effekter och bieffekter. För författaren började liv och minnen försvinna redan medan behandlingarna på »fabriken« pågick. \nLinda Boström Knausgårds tredje roman Oktober barn är en ursinnig uppgörelse med psykiatrin men också ett allt mer desperat minnesarbete, en ojämn kamp mot dörrar som slutgiltigt stängs, där barndom, ungdom, äktenskap, föräldraskap och skilsmässa flimrar förbi, och människor och platser träder fram i blixtbelysning. En förtvivlad bok helt utan försvar, en berättelse som i grund och botten bara vill finnas kvar. \n\nLINDA BOSTRÖM KNAUSGÅRD debuterade som poet 1998. Hennes första roman, Helioskatastrofen (2013), belönades med Mare Kandre-priset. Romanen Välkommen till Amerika nominerades till Augustpriset 2016 och har översatts till tretton språk.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16795852604113564318\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18034141134798434259">Nominated to the 2004 Nordic Council’s Literature Prize & awarded the 2004 Norwegian Critics’ Prize.<br/><br/>Karl Ove Knausgård (b. 1968) made his literary debut in 1998 with the widely acclaimed novel <i>Out of the World</i>, which was a great critical and commercial success and won him, as the first debut novel ever, The Norwegian Critics' Prize. He then went on to write six autobiographical novels, titled <i>My S</i></span>
  <span id="freeText18034141134798434259" style="display:none">Nominated to the 2004 Nordic Council’s Literature Prize &amp; awarded the 2004 Norwegian Critics’ Prize.<br /><br />Karl Ove Knausgård (b. 1968) made his literary debut in 1998 with the widely acclaimed novel <i>Out of the World</i>, which was a great critical and commercial success and won him, as the first debut novel ever, The Norwegian Critics' Prize. He then went on to write six autobiographical novels, titled <i>My Struggle</i> (<i>Min Kamp</i>), which have become a publication phenomenon in his native Norway as well as the world over.</span>

Med <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5362426629054426842\" style=\"display:none\">Femte bind av den selvbiografiske romanen Min kamp er skrevet med voldsom kraft og oppriktighet. Karl Ove flytter til Bergen for å gå på Skrivekunstakademiet. Det blir en gedigen skuffelse; han vil så mye, skjønner så lite, og får ingenting til. Med et tilsynelatende grunnløst pågangsmot fortsetter han likevel å skrive og lese. Flere av de han kjenner, blir antatt og debuterer som forfattere, og han begynner å tenke at han selv i beste fall kan bli en habil litteraturskribent, men ikke kunstner. Gradvis endrer skrivingen seg. Forholdet til verden rundt ham endrer seg også. Slik blir dette en roman om nye, sterke vennskap, og et alvorlig, omskakende kjærlighetsforhold, inntil romanen igjen når frem til det eksistensielle omdreiningspunktet som kanskje er den avgjørende impulsen for romanserien: faren dør, Karl Ove debuterer som forfatter og alt bryter sammen. Karl Ove Knausgårds tredje roman innebærer en enorm litterær satsning, og er en stor bok i mer enn én forstand: Min kamp blir utgitt som seks romaner. Første, andre, tredje og fjerde bok er utkommet, den sjette utkommer høsten 2010.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"5362426629054426842\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return
